</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><sup>233</sup>U is fissile and can be used as a nuclear fuel in the same way as <sup>235</sup>U or <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239"><sup>239</sup>Pu</a>. When <sup>233</sup>U undergoes nuclear fission, the neutrons emitted can strike further <sup>232</sup>Th nuclei, continuing the cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200652–53_31-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200652–53-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This parallels the uranium fuel cycle in <a href="/wiki/Fast_breeder_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast breeder reactor">fast breeder reactors</a> where <sup>238</sup>U undergoes neutron capture to become <sup>239</sup>U, beta decaying to first <sup>239</sup>Np and then fissile <sup>239</sup>Pu.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages">Advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Advantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thorium is more abundant than uranium and can satisfy world energy demands for longer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw19971259_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw19971259-156">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>232</sup>Th absorbs neutrons more readily than <sup>238</sup>U, and <sup>233</sup>U has a higher probability of fission upon neutron capture (92.0%) than <sup>235</sup>U (85.5%) or <sup>239</sup>Pu (73.5%).<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> It also releases more neutrons upon fission on average.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw19971259_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw19971259-156">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> A single neutron capture by <sup>238</sup>U produces transuranic waste along with the fissile <sup>239</sup>Pu, but <sup>232</sup>Th only produces this waste after five captures, forming <sup>237</sup>Np. This number of captures does not happen for 98–99% of the <sup>232</sup>Th nuclei because the intermediate products <sup>233</sup>U or <sup>235</sup>U undergo fission, and fewer long-lived transuranics are produced. Because of this, thorium is a potentially attractive alternative to uranium in <a href="/wiki/MOX_fuel" title="MOX fuel">mixed oxide fuels</a> to minimise the generation of transuranics and maximise the destruction of plutonium.<sup id="cite_ref-wnn-20130621_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20130621-158">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thorium fuels also result in a safer and better-performing <a href="/wiki/Reactor_core" class="mw-redirect" title="Reactor core">reactor core</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200652–53_31-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200652–53-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> because thorium dioxide has a higher melting point, higher <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, and a lower <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_thermal_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Coefficient of thermal expansion">coefficient of thermal expansion</a> and is more stable chemically than the now-common fuel uranium dioxide, which can further oxidise to <a href="/wiki/Triuranium_octoxide" title="Triuranium octoxide">triuranium octoxide</a> (U<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-Thorium_Fuel_Cycle_–_Potential_Benefits_and_Challenges_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thorium_Fuel_Cycle_–_Potential_Benefits_and_Challenges-159">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The used fuel is difficult and dangerous to reprocess because many of the daughters of <sup>232</sup>Th and <sup>233</sup>U are strong gamma emitters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw19971259_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw19971259-156">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> All <sup>233</sup>U production methods result in impurities of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-232" title="Uranium-232"><sup>232</sup>U</a>, either from parasitic knock-out (n,2n) reactions on <sup>232</sup>Th, <sup>233</sup>Pa, or <sup>233</sup>U that result in the loss of a neutron, or from double neutron capture of <sup>230</sup>Th, an impurity in natural <sup>232</sup>Th:<sup id="cite_ref-Intro2WMD_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intro2WMD-160">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">230</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">90</sub></span>Th<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + n → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">231</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">90</sub></span>Th<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="texhtml">γ</span> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;"><i>β</i><sup>−</sup></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">→</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">25.5 h</span></span></span> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">231</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">91</sub></span>Pa<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;"><i>α</i></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">→</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">3.28 × 10<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span> y</span></span></span> &#173; <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">231</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">91</sub></span>Pa<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + n → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">232</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">91</sub></span>Pa<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="texhtml">γ</span> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;"><i>β</i><sup>−</sup></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">→</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">1.3 d</span></span></span> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">232</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">92</sub></span>U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p><sup>232</sup>U by itself is not particularly harmful, but quickly decays to produce the strong gamma emitter <sup>208</sup>Tl. (<sup>232</sup>Th follows the same decay chain, but its much longer half-life means that the quantities of <sup>208</sup>Tl produced are negligible.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200530_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200530-161">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> These impurities of <sup>232</sup>U make <sup>233</sup>U easy to detect and dangerous to work on, and the impracticality of their separation limits the possibilities of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a> using <sup>233</sup>U as the fissile material.<sup id="cite_ref-Intro2WMD_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intro2WMD-160">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> <sup>233</sup>Pa has a relatively long half-life of 27&#160;days and a high <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a> for neutron capture. Thus it is a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_poison" title="Neutron poison">neutron poison</a>: instead of rapidly decaying to the useful <sup>233</sup>U, a significant amount of <sup>233</sup>Pa converts to <sup>234</sup>U and consumes neutrons, degrading <a href="/wiki/Neutron_economy" title="Neutron economy">the reactor efficiency</a>. To avoid this, <sup>233</sup>Pa is extracted from the active zone of thorium <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">molten salt reactors</a> during their operation, so that it only decays to <sup>233</sup>U.<sup id="cite_ref-NakajimaGroult2005_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NakajimaGroult2005-162">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The irradiation of <sup>232</sup>Th with neutrons, followed by its processing, need to be mastered before these advantages can be realised, and this requires more advanced technology than the uranium and plutonium fuel cycle;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200652–53_31-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200652–53-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> research continues in this area. Others cite the low commercial viability of the thorium fuel cycle:<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SovacoolValentine2012_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SovacoolValentine2012-164">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> the international <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">Nuclear Energy Agency</a> predicts that the thorium cycle will never be commercially viable while uranium is available in abundance—a situation which may persist "in the coming decades".<sup id="cite_ref-FindlayFindlay2011_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FindlayFindlay2011-166">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The isotopes produced in the thorium fuel cycle are mostly not transuranic, but some of them are still very dangerous, such as <sup>231</sup>Pa, which has a half-life of 32,760&#160;years and is a major contributor to the long-term <a href="/wiki/Radiotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiotoxic">radiotoxicity</a> of spent nuclear fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-NakajimaGroult2005_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NakajimaGroult2005-162">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" class="image"><img alt="Thorium mantle installed over a small sprout of grass" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/220px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/330px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png/440px-PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png 2x" data-file-width="1831" data-file-height="1237" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PSM_V74_D233_Thorium_radioactive_incandescent_gas_mantle_placed_above_plant_seeds.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Experiment on the effect of radiation (from an unburned thorium gas mantle) on the germination and growth of <a href="/wiki/Timothy-grass" title="Timothy-grass">timothy-grass</a> seed</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiological">Radiological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Radiological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural thorium decays very slowly compared to many other radioactive materials, and the emitted <a href="/wiki/Alpha_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpha radiation">alpha radiation</a> cannot penetrate human skin. As a result, handling small amounts of thorium, such as those in gas mantles, is considered safe, although the use of such items may pose some risks.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Exposure to an aerosol of thorium, such as contaminated dust, can lead to increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, as lungs and other internal organs can be penetrated by alpha radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Exposure to thorium internally leads to increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-arpansa_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arpansa-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The decay products of <sup>232</sup>Th include more dangerous radionuclides such as radium and radon. Although relatively little of those products are created as the result of the slow decay of thorium, a proper assessment of the radiological toxicity of <sup>232</sup>Th must include the contribution of its daughters, some of which are dangerous <a href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamma radiation">gamma</a> emitters,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> and which are built up quickly following the initial decay of <sup>232</sup>Th due to the absence of long-lived nuclides along the decay chain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200535_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200535-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> As the dangerous daughters of thorium have much lower melting points than thorium dioxide, they are volatilised every time the mantle is heated for use. In the first hour of use large fractions of the thorium daughters <sup>224</sup>Ra, <sup>228</sup>Ra, <sup>212</sup>Pb, and <sup>212</sup>Bi are released.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Most of the radiation dose by a normal user arises from inhaling the radium, resulting in a radiation dose of up to 0.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">millisieverts</a> per use, about a third of the dose sustained during a <a href="/wiki/Mammogram" class="mw-redirect" title="Mammogram">mammogram</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear safety">nuclear safety</a> agencies make recommendations about the use of thorium mantles and have raised safety concerns regarding their <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle#Safety_concerns" title="Gas mantle">manufacture</a> and disposal; the radiation dose from one mantle is not a serious problem, but that from many mantles gathered together in factories or landfills is.<sup id="cite_ref-arpansa_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arpansa-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological">Biological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Biological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thorium is odourless and tasteless.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The chemical toxicity of thorium is low because thorium and its most common compounds (mostly the dioxide) are poorly soluble in water,<sup id="cite_ref-Schneckenstein_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneckenstein-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> precipitating out before entering the body as the hydroxide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200534_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200534-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Some thorium compounds are chemically moderately <a href="/wiki/Toxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic">toxic</a>, especially in the presence of strong complex-forming ions such as citrate that carry the thorium into the body in soluble form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200535_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200535-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> If a thorium-containing object has been chewed or sucked, it loses 0.4% of thorium and 90% of its dangerous daughters to the body.<sup id="cite_ref-Poljanc_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poljanc-124">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Three quarters of the thorium that has penetrated the body accumulates in the <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>. Absorption through the skin is possible, but is not a likely means of exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_167-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Thorium's low solubility in water also means that excretion of thorium by the kidneys and faeces is rather slow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200535_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200535-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tests on the thorium uptake of workers involved in monazite processing showed thorium levels above recommended limits in their bodies, but no adverse effects on health were found at those moderately low concentrations. No chemical toxicity has yet been observed in the <a href="/wiki/Tracheobronchial_tract" class="mw-redirect" title="Tracheobronchial tract">tracheobronchial tract</a> and the lungs from exposure to thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200534_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200534-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> People who work with thorium compounds are at a risk of <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>. It can take as much as thirty years after the ingestion of thorium for symptoms to manifest themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_51-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-51">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Thorium has no known biological role.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_51-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-51">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Chemical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Powdered thorium metal is pyrophoric: it ignites spontaneously in air.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200661–63_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200661–63-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In 1964, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">United States Department of the Interior</a> listed thorium as "severe" on a table entitled "Ignition and explosibility of metal powders". Its ignition temperature was given as 270&#160;°C (520&#160;°F) for dust clouds and 280&#160;°C (535&#160;°F) for layers. Its minimum explosive concentration was listed as 0.075&#160;oz/cu ft (0.075&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup>); the minimum igniting energy for (non-submicron) dust was listed as 5&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">mJ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1956, the <a href="/wiki/Sylvania_Electric_Products_explosion" title="Sylvania Electric Products explosion">Sylvania Electric Products explosion</a> occurred during reprocessing and burning of thorium sludge in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, United States. Nine people were injured; one died of complications caused by <a href="/wiki/Third-degree_burns" class="mw-redirect" title="Third-degree burns">third-degree burns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AP_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Newsday_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsday-179">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exposure_routes">Exposure routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Exposure routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thorium exists in very small quantities everywhere on Earth although larger amounts exist in certain parts: the average human contains about 40&#160;<a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> of thorium and typically consumes three micrograms per day.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_51-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-51">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Most thorium exposure occurs through dust inhalation; some thorium comes with food and water, but because of its low solubility, this exposure is negligible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoll200535_170-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoll200535-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure is raised for people who live near thorium deposits or radioactive waste disposal sites, those who live near or work in uranium, phosphate, or tin processing factories, and for those who work in gas mantle production.<sup id="cite_ref-ATSDR_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-180">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Thorium is especially common in the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> coastal areas of India, where residents may be exposed to a naturally occurring radiation dose ten times higher than the worldwide average.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> It is also common in northern Brazilian coastal areas, from south Bahia to <a href="/wiki/Guarapari" title="Guarapari">Guarapari</a>, a city with highly radioactive <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">Monazite</a> sand beaches, with radiation levels up to 50 times higher than world average background radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thorium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">Bismuth</a> is very slightly radioactive, but its half-life (1.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">19</span></sup> years) is so long that its decay is negligible even over geological timespans.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While <a href="/wiki/Einsteinium" title="Einsteinium">einsteinium</a> has been measured to have a lower density, this measurement was done on small, microgram-mass samples, and is likely because of the rapid self-destruction of the crystal structure caused by einsteinium's extreme radioactivity.<sup id="cite_ref-ES_METALL_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ES_METALL-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Behind <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200661–63_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200661–63-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> higher boiling points are speculated to be found in the 6d transition metals, but they have not been produced in large enough quantities to test this prediction.<sup id="cite_ref-BFricke_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BFricke-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gamma rays are distinguished by their origin in the nucleus, not their wavelength; hence there is no lower limit to gamma energy derived from radioactive decay.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-fissionable-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-fissionable_30-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-fissionable_30-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">A <i>fissionable</i> nuclide is capable of undergoing fission (even with a low probability) after capturing a high-energy neutron. Some of these nuclides can be induced to fission with low-energy thermal neutrons with a high probability; they are referred to as <i>fissile</i>. A <i>fertile</i> nuclide is one that could be bombarded with neutrons to produce a fissile nuclide. <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">Critical mass</a> is the mass of a ball of a material which could undergo a sustained <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The name <i>ionium</i> for <sup>230</sup>Th is a remnant from a period when different isotopes were not recognised to be the same element and were given different names.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Unlike the previous similarity between the actinides and the transition metals, the main-group similarity largely ends at thorium before being resumed in the second half of the actinide series, because of the growing contribution of the 5f orbitals to covalent bonding. The only other commonly-encountered actinide, uranium, retains some echoes of main-group behaviour. The chemistry of uranium is more complicated than that of thorium, but the two most common oxidation states of uranium are uranium(VI) and uranium(IV); these are two oxidation units apart, with the higher oxidation state corresponding to formal loss of all valence electrons, which is similar to the behaviour of the heavy main-group elements in the <a href="/wiki/P-block" class="mw-redirect" title="P-block">p-block</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-King_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Even_and_odd_atomic_nuclei" title="Even and odd atomic nuclei">An even number of either protons or neutrons</a> generally increases nuclear stability of isotopes, compared to isotopes with odd numbers. Elements with odd atomic numbers have no more than two stable isotopes; even-numbered elements have multiple stable isotopes, with tin (element 50) having ten.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other isotopes may occur alongside <sup>232</sup>Th, but only in trace quantities. If the source contains no uranium, the only other thorium isotope present would be <sup>228</sup>Th, which occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of <sup>232</sup>Th (the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Thorium series">thorium series</a>): the ratio of <sup>228</sup>Th to <sup>232</sup>Th would be under 10<sup>−10</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200653–55_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200653–55-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> If uranium is present, tiny traces of several other isotopes will also be present: <sup>231</sup>Th and <sup>227</sup>Th from the decay chain of <sup>235</sup>U (the <a href="/wiki/Actinium_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Actinium series">actinium series</a>), and slightly larger but still tiny traces of <sup>234</sup>Th and <sup>230</sup>Th from the decay chain of <sup>238</sup>U (the <a href="/wiki/Uranium_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Uranium series">uranium series</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200653–55_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200653–55-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <sup>229</sup>Th is also been produced in the decay chain of <sup>237</sup>Np (the <a href="/wiki/Neptunium_series" class="mw-redirect" title="Neptunium series">neptunium series</a>): all primordial <sup>237</sup>Np is <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct</a>, but it is still produced as a result of nuclear reactions in uranium ores.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <sup>229</sup>Th is mostly produced as a <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">daughter</a> of artificial <sup>233</sup>U made by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_irradiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron irradiation">neutron irradiation</a> of <sup>232</sup>Th, and is extremely rare in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200653–55_21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200653–55-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thorianite refers to minerals with 75–100&#160;mol%&#160;ThO<sub>2</sub>; uranothorianite, 25–75&#160;mol%&#160;ThO<sub>2</sub>; thorian uraninite, 15–25&#160;mol%&#160;ThO<sub>2</sub>; <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">uraninite</a>, 0–15&#160;mol%&#160;ThO<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200655–56_83-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicklederFourestDorhout200655–56-83">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the time, the <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_element" title="Rare-earth element">rare-earth elements</a>, among which thorium was found and with which it is closely associated in nature, were thought to be divalent; the rare earths were given <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> values two-thirds of their actual ones, and thorium and uranium are given values half of the actual ones.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The main difficulty in isolating thorium lies not in its chemical electropositivity, but in the close association of thorium in nature with the rare-earth elements and uranium, which collectively are difficult to separate from each other. Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/Lars_Fredrik_Nilson" title="Lars Fredrik Nilson">Lars Fredrik Nilson</a>, the discoverer of scandium, had previously made an attempt to isolate thorium metal in 1882, but was unsuccessful at achieving a high degree of purity.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Lely and Hamburger obtained 99% pure thorium metal by reducing thorium chloride with sodium metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Meister_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meister-103">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> A simpler method leading to even higher purity was discovered in 1927 by American engineers John Marden and Harvey Rentschler, involving the reduction of thorium oxide with calcium in presence of calcium chloride.<sup id="cite_ref-Meister_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meister-103">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thorium also appears in the 1864 table by British chemist <a href="/wiki/John_Newlands_(chemist)" title="John Newlands (chemist)">John Newlands</a> as the last and heaviest element, as it was initially thought that uranium was a trivalent element with an atomic weight of around 120: this is half of its actual value, since uranium is predominantly hexavalent. It also appears as the heaviest element in the 1864 table by British chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Odling" title="William Odling">William Odling</a> under titanium, zirconium, and <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a>. It does not appear in the periodic systems published by French geologist <a href="/wiki/Alexandre-%C3%89mile_B%C3%A9guyer_de_Chancourtois" title="Alexandre-Émile Béguyer de Chancourtois">Alexandre-Émile Béguyer de Chancourtois</a> in 1862, German-American musician <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Hinrichs" title="Gustav Hinrichs">Gustav Hinrichs</a> in 1867, or German chemist <a href="/wiki/Julius_Lothar_Meyer" title="Julius Lothar Meyer">Julius Lothar Meyer</a> in 1870, all of which exclude the rare earths and thorium.<sup id="cite_ref-leach_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leach-105">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The filling of the 5f subshell from the beginning of the actinide series was confirmed in 1964 when the next element, <a href="/wiki/Rutherfordium" title="Rutherfordium">rutherfordium</a>, was first synthesised and found to behave like hafnium, as would be expected if the filling of the 5f orbitals had already finished by then.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Today, thorium's similarities to hafnium are still sometimes acknowledged by calling it a "pseudo group 4 element".<sup id="cite_ref-Pershina_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pershina-119">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The thirteen fissile actinide isotopes with half-lives over a year are <sup>229</sup>Th, <sup>233</sup>U, <sup>235</sup>U, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium-236" class="mw-redirect" title="Neptunium-236"><sup>236</sup>Np</a>, <sup>239</sup>Pu, <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-241" title="Plutonium-241"><sup>241</sup>Pu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Americium-242m" class="mw-redirect" title="Americium-242m"><sup>242m</sup>Am</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium-243" class="mw-redirect" title="Curium-243"><sup>243</sup>Cm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium-245" class="mw-redirect" title="Curium-245"><sup>245</sup>Cm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curium-247" class="mw-redirect" title="Curium-247"><sup>247</sup>Cm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Californium-249" class="mw-redirect" title="Californium-249"><sup>249</sup>Cf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Californium-251" class="mw-redirect" title="Californium-251"><sup>251</sup>Cf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Einsteinium-252" class="mw-redirect" title="Einsteinium-252"><sup>252</sup>Es</a>. Of these, only <sup>235</sup>U is naturally occurring, and only <sup>233</sup>U and <sup>239</sup>Pu can be bred from naturally occurring nuclei with a single neutron capture.<sup id="cite_ref-anucene_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anucene-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Gale, W. F.; Totemeier, T. C. (2003). <i>Smithells Metals Reference Book</i>. <a href="/wiki/Butterworth-Heinemann" title="Butterworth-Heinemann">Butterworth-Heinemann</a>. pp.&#160;15–2–15–3. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-08-048096-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-08-048096-1">978-0-08-048096-1</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Smithells+Metals+Reference+Book&amp;rft.pages=15-2-15-3&amp;rft.pub=Butterworth-Heinemann&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-08-048096-1&amp;rft.aulast=Gale&amp;rft.aufirst=W.+F.&amp;rft.au=Totemeier%2C+T.+C.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThorium" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Yu._D._Tretyakov-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Yu._D._Tretyakov_7-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Tretyakov, Yu. Academy. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-5-7695-2533-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-5-7695-2533-9">978-5-7695-2533-9</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Non-organic+chemistry+in+three+volumes&amp;rft.series=Chemistry+of+transition+elements&amp;rft.pub=Academy&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-5-7695-2533-9&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AThorium" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
